<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Mexico" title="Economy of Mexico">Economy of Mexico</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Mexico" title="Economic history of Mexico">Economic history of Mexico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="862" data-file-width="1107" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg/350px-Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg/525px-Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg/700px-Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mexico_Product_Export_Treemap.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A proportional representation of Mexico's exports. The country has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_economic_complexity" title="List of countries by economic complexity">most complex economy</a> in Latin America.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mexico has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">15th largest</a> nominal GDP and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">11th largest</a> by <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a>. GDP annual average growth for the period of 1995–2002 was 5.1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crandall_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Crandall-87">[87]</a></sup> Mexico's Gross Domestic Product (GDP) in <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP) was estimated at US $2.2602 trillion in 2015, and $1.3673 trillion in nominal exchange rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-167">[167]</a></sup> Mexico's GDP in PPP per capita was US $18,714.05. The World Bank reported in 2009 that the country's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_National_Income" title="Gross National Income">Gross National Income</a> in market exchange rates was the second highest in Latin America, after <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> at US $1,830.392 billion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GNI_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-GNI-168">[168]</a></sup> which lead to the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_National_Income" title="Gross National Income">income per capita</a> in the region at $14,400.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GNIPC_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-GNIPC-169">[169]</a></sup> Mexico is now firmly established as an upper middle-income country. After the slowdown of 2001 the country has recovered and has grown 4.2, 3.0 and 4.8 percent in 2004, 2005 and 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> even though it is considered to be well below Mexico's potential growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hufbauer_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hufbauer-171">[171]</a></sup> Furthermore, after the 2008–2009 recession, the economy grew an average of 3.32 percent per year from 2010 to 2014.</p>
<p>From the late 1990s onwards, the majority of the population has been part of the growing middle class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WB_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-WB-172">[172]</a></sup> But according to Mexico's <a href="/wiki/National_Council_for_the_Evaluation_of_Social_Development_Policy_(CONEVAL)" title="National Council for the Evaluation of Social Development Policy (CONEVAL)">National Council for the Evaluation of Social Development Policy</a> (CONEVAL) from 2006 to 2010 the portion of the population who were in poverty rose from 19% to 46% (52 million persons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CONEVAL_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-CONEVAL-173">[173]</a></sup> However, rather than Mexico’s economy crashing, international economists attribute the huge increase in the percentage of population living below the country's poverty line to the CONEVAL changing the standards used to define it, pointing out that the percentage of people living in poverty according Mexico’s national poverty line is around 40 times higher than the one reported by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank’s</a> international poverty line, with the difference between the two being the biggest in the world. It is pondered then if it wouldn't be better for countries in the situation of Mexico to adopt more internationalized standards to measure poverty so the numbers obtained could be used to make accurate international comparisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD's</a> own poverty line (defined as the percentage of a country's population who earns half or less of the national median income) 21.5% of the Mexico's population lives in situation of poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECDREFORMA_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECDREFORMA-175">[175]</a></sup> This is also reflected by the fact that infant mortality in Mexico is three times higher than the average among OECD nations, and the literacy levels are in the median range of OECD nations. Nevertheless, according to <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>, by 2050 Mexico will have the 5th largest economy in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a> countries, Mexico has the second highest degree of economic disparity between the extremely poor and extremely rich, after Chile – although it has been falling over the last decade, being only one of few countries in which this is the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The bottom ten percent in the income hierarchy disposes of 1.36% of the country's resources, whereas the upper ten percent dispose of almost 36%. OECD also notes that Mexico's budgeted expenses for poverty alleviation and social development is only about a third of the OECD average – both in absolute and relative numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECDREFORMA_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-OECDREFORMA-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2008 UN report the average income in a typical urbanized area of Mexico was $26,654, while the average income in rural areas just miles away was only $8,403.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Daily minimum wages are set annually being set at $80.04 <a href="/wiki/Mexican_peso" title="Mexican peso">Mexican pesos</a> ($4.5 USD) in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>The electronics industry of Mexico has grown enormously within the last decade. Mexico has the sixth largest electronics industry in the world after <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. Mexico is the second largest exporter of electronics to the United States where it exported $71.4 billion worth of electronics in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prnewswire.com_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-prnewswire.com-180">[180]</a></sup> The Mexican electronics industry is dominated by the manufacture and OEM design of televisions, displays, computers, mobile phones, circuit boards, semiconductors, electronic appliances, communications equipment and LCD modules. The Mexican electronics industry grew 20% between 2010 and 2011, up from its constant growth rate of 17% between 2003 and 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prnewswire.com_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-prnewswire.com-180">[180]</a></sup> Currently electronics represent 30% of Mexico's exports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prnewswire.com_180-2"><a href="#cite_note-prnewswire.com-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg/220px-Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg/330px-Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg/440px-Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.Distrito.Federal.DF.Paseo.Reforma.Skyline.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a> is the most important financial and economic center in Mexico</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mexico produces the most automobiles of any North American nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> The industry produces technologically complex components and engages in some research and development activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gereffi_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gereffi-182">[182]</a></sup> The "Big Three" (<a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ford" title="Ford">Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>) have been operating in Mexico since the 1930s, while <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a> built their plants in the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hufbauer_Auto_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hufbauer_Auto-183">[183]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puebla,_Puebla" title="Puebla, Puebla">Puebla</a> alone, 70 industrial part-makers cluster around Volkswagen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gereffi_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gereffi-182">[182]</a></sup> In the 2010s expansion of the sector was surging. In 2014 alone, more than $10 billion in investment was committed. Kia Motors in August 2014 announced plans for a $1 billion factory in <a href="/wiki/Nuevo_Le%C3%B3n" title="Nuevo León">Nuevo León</a>. At the time <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a> were already building a $1.4 billion plant near Puebla, while <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> was planning a $1-billion assembly plant in <a href="/wiki/San_Luis_Potos%C3%AD" title="San Luis Potosí">San Luis Potosí</a>. Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Audi" title="Audi">Audi</a> began building a $1.3 billion factory near Puebla in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KiaMexico_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-KiaMexico-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>The domestic car industry is represented by <a href="/wiki/DINA_S.A." title="DINA S.A.">DINA S.A.</a>, which has built buses and trucks since 1962,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> and the new <a href="/wiki/Mastretta" title="Mastretta">Mastretta</a> company that builds the high-performance <a href="/wiki/Mastretta_MXT" title="Mastretta MXT">Mastretta MXT</a> sports car.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> In 2006, trade with the United States and Canada accounted for almost 50% of Mexico's exports and 45% of its imports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia.gov_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia.gov-13">[13]</a></sup> During the first three quarters of 2010, the United States had a $46.0 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_deficit" title="Trade deficit">trade deficit</a> with Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> In August 2010 Mexico surpassed France to become the 9th largest holder of US debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The commercial and financial dependence on the US is a cause for concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>The remittances from Mexican citizens working in the United States account for 0.2% of Mexico's GDP<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> which was equal to US$20 billion per year in 2004 and is the tenth largest source of foreign income after oil, industrial exports, manufactured goods, electronics, heavy industry, automobiles, construction, food, banking and financial services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> According to Mexico's central bank, remittances in 2008 amounted to $25bn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<p>Major players in the broadcasting industry are <a href="/wiki/Televisa" title="Televisa">Televisa</a>, the largest Spanish media company in the Spanish-speaking world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-193">[193]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/TV_Azteca" title="TV Azteca">TV Azteca</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_in_Mexico" title="Telecommunications in Mexico">Telecommunications in Mexico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MORELOS_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1385" data-file-width="1000" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/MORELOS_3.jpg/200px-MORELOS_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/MORELOS_3.jpg/300px-MORELOS_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/MORELOS_3.jpg/400px-MORELOS_3.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MORELOS_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Morelos 3, the latest addition to the <a href="/wiki/Morelos_Satellite_System" title="Morelos Satellite System">Morelos Satellite System</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The telecommunications industry is mostly dominated by <a href="/wiki/Telmex" title="Telmex">Telmex</a> (<i>Teléfonos de México</i>), privatized in 1990. By 2006, Telmex had expanded its operations to Colombia, Peru, Chile, Argentina, Brazil and Uruguay and the United States. Other players in the domestic industry are <a href="/wiki/Axtel" title="Axtel">Axtel</a> and Maxcom. Because of Mexican <a href="/wiki/Orography" title="Orography">orography</a>, providing a landline telephone service at remote mountainous areas is expensive, and the penetration of line-phones per capita is low compared to other Latin American countries, at 40 percent; however, 82% of Mexicans over the age of 14 own a mobile phone. Mobile telephony has the advantage of reaching all areas at a lower cost, and the total number of mobile lines is almost two times that of landlines, with an estimation of 63 million lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ciacom_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-ciacom-194">[194]</a></sup> The telecommunication industry is regulated by the government through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cofetel" title="Cofetel">Cofetel</a> (<i>Comisión Federal de Telecomunicaciones</i>).</p>
<p>The Mexican satellite system is domestic and operates 120 earth stations. There is also extensive microwave radio relay network and considerable use of fiber-optic and coaxial cable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ciacom_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-ciacom-194">[194]</a></sup> Mexican satellites are operated by <i>Satélites Mexicanos</i> (<a href="/wiki/Satmex" title="Satmex">Satmex</a>), a private company, leader in Latin America and servicing both North and South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> It offers broadcast, telephone and telecommunication services to 37 countries in the Americas, from Canada to <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>. Through business partnerships Satmex provides high-speed connectivity to ISPs and Digital Broadcast Services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Satmex maintains its own satellite fleet with most of the fleet being designed and built in Mexico.</p>
<p>The use of radio, television, and Internet in Mexico is prevalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnations_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-encnations-197">[197]</a></sup> There are approximately 1,410 radio broadcast stations and 236 television stations (excluding repeaters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ciacom_194-2"><a href="#cite_note-ciacom-194">[194]</a></sup> Major players in the broadcasting industry are <a href="/wiki/Televisa" title="Televisa">Televisa</a>—the largest media company in the Spanish-speaking world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-193">[193]</a></sup>—and <a href="/wiki/TV_Azteca" title="TV Azteca">TV Azteca</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_Mexico" title="Electricity sector in Mexico">Electricity sector in Mexico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I,_Fase_II.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1104" data-file-width="1654" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I%2C_Fase_II.jpg/200px-Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I%2C_Fase_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I%2C_Fase_II.jpg/300px-Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I%2C_Fase_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I%2C_Fase_II.jpg/400px-Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_Central_E%C3%B3lica_Sureste_I%2C_Fase_II.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Central Eólica Sureste I, Fase II in Oaxaca. The Isthmus of Tehuantepec is the region of Mexico with the highest capacity for wind energy. (see <i><a href="/wiki/Tehuantepecer" title="Tehuantepecer">Tehuantepecer</a></i>, a strong wind that affects the region)</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III,_Fase_1._(21526244958).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3680" data-file-width="5520" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III%2C_Fase_1._%2821526244958%29.jpg/200px-Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III%2C_Fase_1._%2821526244958%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III%2C_Fase_1._%2821526244958%29.jpg/300px-Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III%2C_Fase_1._%2821526244958%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III%2C_Fase_1._%2821526244958%29.jpg/400px-Inauguraci%C3%B3n_de_la_Central_Geotermoel%C3%A9ctrica_Azufres_III%2C_Fase_1._%2821526244958%29.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Central Geotermoeléctrica Azufres III in Michoacán. 100% of the electricity produced in Michoacán comes from renewable sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geothermal_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geothermal-198">[198]</a></sup> 90% comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_plants" title="Hydroelectric plants">hydroelectric plants</a>, and 10% from the Azufres <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">Geothermal</a> Field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geothermal_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geothermal-198">[198]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Energy production in Mexico is managed by state-owned companies: the <a href="/wiki/Comisi%C3%B3n_Federal_de_Electricidad" title="Comisión Federal de Electricidad">Federal Commission of Electricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pemex" title="Pemex">Pemex</a>.</p>
<p>Pemex, the public company in charge of exploration, extraction, transportation and marketing of crude oil and natural gas, as well as the refining and distribution of petroleum products and petrochemicals, is one of the largest companies in the world by revenue, making US $86 billion in sales a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fortune_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-fortune-200">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Mexico is the sixth-largest oil producer in the world, with 3.7 million barrels per day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> In 1980 oil exports accounted for 61.6% of total exports; by 2000 it was only 7.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gereffi_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gereffi-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>The largest hydro plant in Mexico is the 2,400 MW <a href="/wiki/Chicoas%C3%A9n_Dam" title="Chicoasén Dam">Manuel Moreno Torres Dam</a> in Chicoasén, <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Grijalva_River" title="Grijalva River">Grijalva River</a>. This is the world's fourth most productive hydroelectric plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mexico is the country with the world's third largest solar potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S.26G_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-S.26G-204">[204]</a></sup> The country's gross solar potential is estimated at 5kWh/m2 daily, which corresponds to 50 times national electricity generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ER_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-ER-205">[205]</a></sup> Currently, there is over 1 million square meters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_thermal" title="Solar thermal">solar thermal</a> panels<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SENER_2009b_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-SENER_2009b-206">[206]</a></sup> installed in Mexico, while in 2005, there were 115,000 square meters of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_PV" title="Solar PV">solar PV</a> (photo-voltaic). It is expected that in 2012 there will be 1,8 million square meters of installed solar thermal panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SENER_2009b_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-SENER_2009b-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>The project named <a href="/wiki/SEGH-CFE_1" title="SEGH-CFE 1">SEGH-CFE 1</a>, located in Puerto Libertad, Sonora, Northwest of Mexico, will have capacity of 46.8 MW from an array of 187,200 solar panels when complete in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sonora_Energy_Group_Hermosillo_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sonora_Energy_Group_Hermosillo-207">[207]</a></sup> All of the electricity will be sold directly to the CFE and absorbed into the utility's transmission system for distribution throughout their existing network. At an installed capacity of 46.8 MWp, when complete in 2013, the project will be the first utility scale project of its kind in Mexico and the largest solar project of any kind in Latin America.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_Mexico" title="History of science and technology in Mexico">History of science and technology in Mexico</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Autonomous_University_of_Mexico" title="National Autonomous University of Mexico">National Autonomous University of Mexico</a> was officially established in 1910,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> and the university become one of the most important institutes of higher learning in Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-page285_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-page285-209">[209]</a></sup> UNAM provides world class education in science, medicine, and engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Many scientific institutes and new institutes of higher learning, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Polytechnic_Institute" title="National Polytechnic Institute">National Polytechnic Institute</a> (founded in 1936),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> were established during the first half of the 20th century. Most of the new research institutes were created within UNAM. Twelve institutes were integrated into UNAM from 1929 to 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-page18_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-page18-212">[212]</a></sup> In 1959, the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Mexican Academy of Sciences">Mexican Academy of Sciences</a> was created to coordinate scientific efforts between academics.</p>
<p>In 1995, the Mexican chemist <a href="/wiki/Mario_J._Molina" title="Mario J. Molina">Mario J. Molina</a> shared the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> with <a href="/wiki/Paul_J._Crutzen" title="Paul J. Crutzen">Paul J. Crutzen</a> and <a href="/wiki/F._Sherwood_Rowland" title="F. Sherwood Rowland">F. Sherwood Rowland</a> for their work in atmospheric chemistry, particularly concerning the formation and decomposition of ozone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Molina, an alumnus of UNAM, became the first Mexican citizen to win the Nobel Prize in science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent years, the largest scientific project being developed in Mexico was the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Large_Millimeter_Telescope" title="Large Millimeter Telescope">Large Millimeter Telescope</a> (Gran Telescopio Milimétrico, GMT), the world's largest and most sensitive single-aperture telescope in its frequency range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> It was designed to observe regions of space obscured by stellar dust.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Mexico" title="Tourism in Mexico">Tourism in Mexico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios,_Cholula,_Puebla.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="1000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios%2C_Cholula%2C_Puebla.jpg/250px-Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios%2C_Cholula%2C_Puebla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios%2C_Cholula%2C_Puebla.jpg/375px-Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios%2C_Cholula%2C_Puebla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios%2C_Cholula%2C_Puebla.jpg/500px-Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios%2C_Cholula%2C_Puebla.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios,_Cholula,_Puebla.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Iglesia_de_Nuestra_Se%C3%B1ora_de_los_Remedios_(Mexico)" title="Iglesia de Nuestra Señora de los Remedios (Mexico)">Iglesia de Nuestra Señora de los Remedios</a> atop the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Cholula" title="Great Pyramid of Cholula">Great Pyramid of Cholula</a> with <a href="/wiki/Popocat%C3%A9petl" title="Popocatépetl">Popocatépetl</a> visible in the background has become an iconic image of Mexico.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mexico has traditionally been among the <a href="/wiki/World_Tourism_rankings" title="World Tourism rankings">most visited countries in the world</a> according to the World Tourism Organization and it is the most visited country in the Americas, after the United States. The most notable attractions are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican" title="Mesoamerican">Mesoamerican</a> ruins, cultural festivals, colonial cities, nature reserves and the beach resorts. The nation's wide range of climates, from temperate to tropical, and unique culture – a fusion of the European and the Mesoamerican – make Mexico an attractive destination. The peak tourism seasons in the country are during December and the mid-Summer, with brief surges during the week before <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spring_break" title="Spring break">Spring break</a>, when many of the beach resort sites become popular destinations for college students from the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>.</p>
<p>As of 2011, Mexico had the 23rd highest income from tourism in the world, and the highest in Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> The vast majority of tourists come to Mexico from the United States and Canada followed by Europe and Asia. A smaller number also come from other Latin American countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> In the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Travel_and_Tourism_Competitiveness_Report" title="Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Report">Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Index</a> report, Mexico was ranked 43rd in the world, which was 4th in the Americas .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TTCI2011_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-TTCI2011-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="2560" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg/220px-Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg/330px-Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg/440px-Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cozumel_Punta_Sur_View-27527.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Caribbean state of <a href="/wiki/Quintana_Roo" title="Quintana Roo">Quintana Roo</a> is particularly known for beach tourism. Major destinations include Cancún, <a href="/wiki/Cozumel" title="Cozumel">Cozumel</a> and the Riviera Maya.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The coastlines of Mexico harbor many stretches of beaches that are frequented by sun bathers and other visitors. <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Mexico#Article_27" title="Constitution of Mexico">According to national law</a>, the entirety of the coastlines are under federal ownership, that is, all beaches in the country are <a href="/wiki/Public_property" title="Public property">public</a>. On the Yucatán peninsula, one of the most popular beach destinations is the resort town of <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn" title="Cancún">Cancún</a>, especially among <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">university</a> <a href="/wiki/Student" title="Student">students</a> during <a href="/wiki/Spring_break" title="Spring break">spring break</a>. Just offshore is the beach island of <a href="/wiki/Isla_Mujeres" title="Isla Mujeres">Isla Mujeres</a>, and to the east is the <a href="/wiki/Isla_Holbox" title="Isla Holbox">Isla Holbox</a>. To the south of Cancun is the coastal strip called <a href="/wiki/Riviera_Maya" title="Riviera Maya">Riviera Maya</a> which includes the beach town of <a href="/wiki/Playa_del_Carmen" title="Playa del Carmen">Playa del Carmen</a> and the ecological parks of <a href="/wiki/Xcaret" title="Xcaret">Xcaret</a> and Xel-Há. A day trip to the south of Cancún is the historic port of <a href="/wiki/Tulum" title="Tulum">Tulum</a>. In addition to its beaches, the town of Tulum is notable for its cliff-side <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Mayan</a> ruins.</p>
<p>On the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific" title="Pacific">Pacific</a> coast is the notable tourist destination of <a href="/wiki/Acapulco" title="Acapulco">Acapulco</a>. Once the destination for the rich and famous, the beaches have become crowded and the shores are now home to many multi-story hotels and vendors. Acapulco is home to renowned cliff divers: trained divers who leap from the side of a vertical cliff into the surf below.</p>
<p>At the southern tip of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baja_California_peninsula" title="Baja California peninsula">Baja California peninsula</a> is the resort town of <a href="/wiki/Cabo_San_Lucas" title="Cabo San Lucas">Cabo San Lucas</a>, a town noted for its beaches and <a href="/wiki/Marlin" title="Marlin">marlin</a> <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Further north along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_of_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Sea of Cortés">Sea of Cortés</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%ADa_Concepci%C3%B3n,_Baja_California_Sur" title="Bahía Concepción, Baja California Sur">Bahía de La Concepción</a>, another beach town known for its sports fishing. Closer to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> border is the weekend draw of <a href="/wiki/San_Felipe,_Baja_California" title="San Felipe, Baja California">San Felipe, Baja California</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Mexico" title="Transportation in Mexico">Transportation in Mexico</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg/220px-Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg/330px-Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg/440px-Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puente_Baluarte_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Baluarte_Bridge" title="Baluarte Bridge">Baluarte Bridge</a> is the highest cable-stayed bridge in the world, the fifth-highest bridge overall and the highest bridge in the Americas.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The roadway network in Mexico is extensive and all areas in the country are covered by it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nations_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-nations-220">[220]</a></sup> The roadway network in Mexico has an extent of 366,095 km (227,481 mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sctroad_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-sctroad-221">[221]</a></sup> of which 116,802 km (72,577 mi) are paved,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sctroad-2_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-sctroad-2-222">[222]</a></sup> making it the largest paved-roadway network in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> Of these, 10,474 km (6,508 mi) are multi-lane <a href="/wiki/Controlled-access_highway" title="Controlled-access highway">expressways</a>: 9,544 km (5,930 mi) are four-lane highways and the rest have 6 or more lanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sctroad-2_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-sctroad-2-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mexico was one of the first Latin American countries to promote railway development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brit-mex_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-brit-mex-117">[117]</a></sup> and the network covers 30,952 km (19,233 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnations_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-encnations-197">[197]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Secretariat_of_Communications_and_Transportation_(Mexico)" title="Secretariat of Communications and Transportation (Mexico)">Secretary of Communications and Transport</a> of Mexico proposed a high-speed rail link that will transport its passengers from <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a> to <a href="/wiki/Guadalajara" title="Guadalajara">Guadalajara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jalisco" title="Jalisco">Jalisco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-azcentral.com_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-azcentral.com-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> The train, which will travel at 300 kilometres per hour (190 miles per hour),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-systra.com_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-systra.com-226">[226]</a></sup> will allow passengers to travel from Mexico City to Guadalajara in just 2 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-systra.com_226-1"><a href="#cite_note-systra.com-226">[226]</a></sup> The whole project was projected to cost 240 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MXN" title="MXN">pesos</a>, or about 25 billion US$<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-azcentral.com_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-azcentral.com-224">[224]</a></sup> and is being paid for jointly by the Mexican government and the local private sector including the wealthiest man in the world, Mexico's billionaire business tycoon <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Slim" title="Carlos Slim">Carlos Slim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> The government of the state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_(state)" title="Yucatán (state)">Yucatán</a> is also funding the construction of a high speed line connecting the cities of <a href="/wiki/Cozumel" title="Cozumel">Cozumel</a> to <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9rida,_Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Mérida, Yucatán">Mérida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a> and Cancún.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mexico has 233 airports with paved runways; of these, 35 carry 97% of the passenger traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encnations_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-encnations-197">[197]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City_International_Airport" title="Mexico City International Airport">Mexico City International Airport</a> remains the largest in Latin America and the 44th largest in the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> transporting 21 million passengers a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">Water supply and sanitation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Mexico" title="Water supply and sanitation in Mexico">Water supply and sanitation in Mexico</a></div>
<p>Among the achievements is a significant increase in access to piped water supply in urban areas (88% to 93%) as well as in rural areas (50% to 74%) between 1990 and 2010. Additionally, a strong nationwide increase in access to <a href="/wiki/Improved_sanitation" title="Improved sanitation">improved sanitation</a> (64% to 85%) was observed in the same period. Other achievements include the existence of a functioning national system to finance water and sanitation infrastructure with a National Water Commission as its apex institution; and the existence of a few well-performing utilities such as Aguas y Drenaje de Monterrey.</p>
<p>The challenges include water scarcity in the northern and central parts of the country; inadequate water service quality (drinking water quality; 11% of Mexicans receiving water only intermittently as of 2014);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> poor technical and commercial efficiency of most utilities (with an average level of <a href="/wiki/Non-revenue_water" title="Non-revenue water">non-revenue water</a> of 43.2% in 2010);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> an insufficient share of wastewater receiving treatment (36% in 2006); and still inadequate access in rural areas. In addition to on-going investments to expand access, the government has embarked on a large investment program to improve wastewater treatment.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Mexico" title="Demographics of Mexico">Demographics of Mexico</a></div>

</table>
<p>According to estimations made by Mexico's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Instituto_Nacional_de_Estad%C3%ADstica_y_Geograf%C3%ADa" title="Instituto Nacional de Estadística y Geografía">National Geography and Statistics Institute</a>, as of 2017 Mexico has 123.5 million inhabitants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> making it the most populous Spanish-speaking country in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spanishhistory_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spanishhistory-234">[234]</a></sup> Between 2005 and 2010, the Mexican population grew at an average of 1.70% per year, up from 1.16% per year between 2000 and 2005.</p>
<p>Even though Mexico is a very ethnically diverse country, research about ethnicity has largely been a forgotten field, in consequence of the post-revolutionary efforts of Mexico's government to unify all Mexicans under a single ethnic identity (that of the "Mestizo"). As a result, since 1930 the only ethnic classification that has been included in Mexican censuses has been that of "Indigenous peoples".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EL_MESTIZAJE_Y_LAS_CULTURAS_REGIONALES_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-EL_MESTIZAJE_Y_LAS_CULTURAS_REGIONALES-235">[235]</a></sup> Even then, across the years the government has used different criteria to count Indigenous peoples, with each of them returning considerably different numbers. It is not until very recently that the Mexican government begun conducting surveys that considered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Mexican" title="Afro-Mexican">Afro-Mexican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexicans_of_European_descent" title="Mexicans of European descent">Euro-Mexican</a> population that lives in the country.</p>
<p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mexico&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the foreign-born population was 1,007,063.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The majority of these individuals were born in the United States and Mexico is home to the <a href="/wiki/American_immigration_to_Mexico" title="American immigration to Mexico">largest number of U.S. citizens abroad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S._237-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S.-237">[237]</a></sup> After Americans the largest immigrant groups are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guatemalan_immigration_to_Mexico" title="Guatemalan immigration to Mexico">Guatemalans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spaniards_in_Mexico" title="Spaniards in Mexico">Spaniards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colombian_Mexicans" title="Colombian Mexicans">Colombians</a>. Besides the Spanish, large immigrant-descended groups are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_immigration_to_Mexico" title="French immigration to Mexico">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_Mexicans" title="German Mexicans">Germans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Mexicans" title="Lebanese Mexicans">Lebanese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Mexicans" title="Chinese Mexicans">Chinese</a>. Mexico is the largest source of immigration to the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> Some 11.6 million residents of the United States have Mexican citizenship as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mexico&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnicity_and_race">Ethnicity and race</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:131px;max-width:131px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:164px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india,_mestiza.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="995" data-file-width="777" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india%2C_mestiza.jpg/129px-De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india%2C_mestiza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india%2C_mestiza.jpg/194px-De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india%2C_mestiza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india%2C_mestiza.jpg/258px-De_espa%C3%B1ol_e_india%2C_mestiza.jpg 2x" width="129"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>De español e india, mestiza</i>, 1763,<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Miguel_Cabrera_(painter)" title="Miguel Cabrera (painter)">Miguel Cabrera</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:129px;max-width:129px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:164px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra,_mulata.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="825" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra%2C_mulata.jpg/127px-De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra%2C_mulata.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra%2C_mulata.jpg/191px-De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra%2C_mulata.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra%2C_mulata.jpg/254px-De_espa%C3%B1ol_y_negra%2C_mulata.jpg 2x" width="127"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>De español y negra, mulata</i>, 1763,<br/>
Miguel Cabrera</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:126px;max-width:126px">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:164px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:De_negro_e_india,_china_cambuja.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="975" data-file-width="736" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/De_negro_e_india%2C_china_cambuja.jpg/124px-De_negro_e_india%2C_china_cambuja.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/De_negro_e_india%2C_china_cambuja.jpg/186px-De_negro_e_india%2C_china_cambuja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/De_negro_e_india%2C_china_cambuja.jpg/248px-De_negro_e_india%2C_china_cambuja.jpg 2x" width="124"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>De negro e india, zamba</i>, 1763,<br/>
Miguel Cabrera</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Depictions of the three main <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castas" title="Castas">castas</a></i> resulting from miscegenation of Europeans, Africans and indigenous Americans. Produced during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_Enlightenment" title="Spanish American Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, the <i>pinturas de castas</i> attempted to "rationally categorize" the racial diversity of Mexico, but some sets were also manifestations of early national self-pride. Wealth and order depicted through clothing, mercantile goods and religious symbols, challenged preconceived notions of New Spain in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mexico is ethnically diverse; with people of several ethnicities being united under a single national identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated115_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated115-241">[241]</a></sup> The core part of Mexican national identity is formed on the basis of a synthesis of cultures, primarily European culture and indigenous cultures, in a process known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mestizaje" title="Mestizaje">mestizaje</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated115_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated115-241">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-census-242">[242]</a></sup> Mexican politicians and reformers such as <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Vasconcelos" title="José Vasconcelos">José Vasconcelos</a> (promoter of the <i><a href="/wiki/La_Raza_C%C3%B3smica" title="La Raza Cósmica">cosmic race</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Gamio" title="Manuel Gamio">Manuel Gamio</a> (promoter of <i><a href="/wiki/Indigenismo" title="Indigenismo">indigenismo</a></i>) were instrumental in building a Mexican national identity on the concept of mestizaje.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Knight.2C_Alan_1990._pp._78_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-Knight.2C_Alan_1990._pp._78-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>The large majority of Mexicans have historically been classified as "<a href="/wiki/Mestizos_in_Mexico" title="Mestizos in Mexico">Mestizos</a>". In modern Mexican usage, the term <i>mestizo</i> is primarily a cultural identity rather than the racial identity it was during the colonial era, resulting in individuals with varying phenotypes being classified under the same identity, regardless of wheter they are of mixed ancestry or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redalyc_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-redalyc-244">[244]</a></sup> Since the term carries a variety of different socio-cultural, economic, racial and biological meanings, it was deemed too imprecise to be used for ethnic classification, thus it was abandoned by the government and is not in wide use in Mexican society,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brit-mex_117-4"><a href="#cite_note-brit-mex-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> although it's often used in literature about Mexican social identities and on intellectual circles. In the Yucatán peninsula the word Mestizo has historically had a different meaning, being used to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Yucatec_Maya_language" title="Yucatec Maya language">Maya</a>-speaking populations living in traditional communities, because during the <a href="/wiki/Caste_War_of_Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Caste War of Yucatán">Caste War</a> of the late 19th century those Maya who did not join the rebellion were classified as mestizos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartolom.C3.A9_1996:2_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartolom.C3.A9_1996:2-246">[246]</a></sup> In Chiapas the word "Ladino" is used instead of mestizo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a> racially Mestizo Mexicans make up 50% to 67% of the country's population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-248">[248]</a></sup></p>
<p>The total percentage of Mexico's population who is <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Mexico" title="Indigenous peoples of Mexico">indigenous</a> varies considerably depending of the criteria used by the government on its censuses: it is 5.4% if the hability to speak an indigenous language is used as the criteria to define a person as indigenous,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegiindigenous_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-inegiindigenous-249">[249]</a></sup> if racial self-identification is used it's 14.9%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDI1_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDI1-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup> and if people who considers themselves part indigenous are also included it amounts to 21.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beta.inegi.org.mx_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-beta.inegi.org.mx-252">[252]</a></sup> Nonetheless all the censuses conclude that the majority of Mexico's indigenous population is concentrated in the southern and south-eastern Mexican states, primarily in rural areas. Some indigenous communities have a degree of autonomy under the legislation of "usos y costumbres", which allows them to regulate some internal issues under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Customary_law" title="Customary law">customary law</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Commission_for_the_Development_of_Indigenous_Peoples" title="National Commission for the Development of Indigenous Peoples">National Commission for the Development of Indigenous Peoples</a>, the states with the greatest proportion of indigenous residents are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Yucatán at 59%, Quintana Roo 39% and Campeche 27%, chiefly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_people" title="Maya people">Maya</a>; Oaxaca with 48% of the population, the most numerous groups being the <a href="/wiki/Mixtec" title="Mixtec">Mixtec</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zapotec_people" title="Zapotec people">Zapotec peoples</a>; Chiapas at 28%, the majority being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tzeltal" title="Tzeltal">Tzeltal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tzotzil_people" title="Tzotzil people">Tzotzil</a> Maya; Hidalgo 24%, the majority being <a href="/wiki/Otomi_people" title="Otomi people">Otomi</a>; Puebla 19%, and Guerrero 17%, mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahua_people" title="Nahua people">Nahua peoples</a> and the states of San Luis Potosí and Veracruz both home to a population that is 15% indigenous, mostly from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totonac" title="Totonac">Totonac</a>, Nahua and <a href="/wiki/Huastec_people" title="Huastec people">Teenek (Huastec)</a> groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegi.org.mx_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-inegi.org.mx-254">[254]</a></sup> The absolute numbers of the indigenous population growing, but at a slower rate than the rest of the population so that the percentage of indigenous peoples in regards to total population is nonetheless falling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDI_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDI-255">[255]</a></sup> All of the indices of social development for the indigenous population are considerably lower than the national average. In all states indigenous people have higher <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a>, in some states almost double of the non-indigenous populations. Literacy rates are also much lower, with 27% of indigenous children between 6 and 14 being illiterate compared to a national average of 12%. The indigenous population participate in the workforce longer than the national average, starting earlier and continuing longer. However, 55% of the indigenous population receive less than a minimum salary, compared to 20% for the national average. Many practice subsistence agriculture and receive no salaries. Indigenous people also have less access to health care and a lower quality of housing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegi.org.mx_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-inegi.org.mx-254">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>Similarly to Mestizo and Indigenous peoples, estimations for the percentage of <a href="/wiki/Mexicans_of_European_descent" title="Mexicans of European descent">European-descended Mexicans</a> within the Mexican population vary considerably: their numbers range from around 10%–20% according to the Encyclopædia Britannica<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-248">[248]</a></sup> to as high as 47%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marzo_DiaIntElimDiscRacial_INACCSS_2017_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marzo_DiaIntElimDiscRacial_INACCSS_2017-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conapred.org.mx_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-conapred.org.mx-257">[257]</a></sup> according to a nationwide survey conducted by Mexico's government, made with the intent of having a precise outlook of the social and economic inequalities that exist between light skinned European looking Mexicans and Indigenous or African looking Mexicans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DISC-RACIAL_2011_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-DISC-RACIAL_2011-258">[258]</a></sup> is the first time the Mexican government has conducted an official population study that referenced Mexico's white population in nearly a century.<br/>
While during the colonial era, most of the European migration into Mexico was <a href="/wiki/Spaniards_in_Mexico" title="Spaniards in Mexico">Spanish</a>, in the 19th and 20th centuries a substantial number of non-Spanish Europeans immigrated to the country. According to 20th and 21st century academics, large scale intermixing between European immigrants and native Indigenous peoples would produce a Mestizo group which would become the overwhelming majority of Mexico's population by the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_revolution" title="Mexican revolution">Mexican revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fnavarrete_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-fnavarrete-259">[259]</a></sup> However, according to church registers from the colonial times, the majority of European men married with European women. Said registers also put in question other narratives held by contemporary academics, such as European migrants who arrived to Mexico being almost exclusively men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scielo.org.mx2_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-scielo.org.mx2-260">[260]</a></sup> Nowadays Mexico’s northern and western regions have the highest European populations, with the majority of the people not having native admixture or being of predominantly European ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UnitedStatesandMexico_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-UnitedStatesandMexico-261">[261]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Mexican" title="Afro-Mexican">Afro-Mexican</a> population (1,381,853 individuals as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mexico&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encuesta_Intercensal_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encuesta_Intercensal-262">[262]</a></sup> is an ethnic group made up of descendants of Colonial-era slaves and recent immigrants of sub-Saharan African descent. Mexico had an active slave trade during the colonial period and some 200,000 Africans were taken there, primarily in the 17th century. The creation of a national Mexican identity, especially after the Mexican Revolution, emphasized Mexico's indigenous and European past; it passively eliminated the African ancestors and contributions. Most of the African-descended population was absorbed into the surrounding Mestizo (mixed European/indigenous) and indigenous populations through unions among the groups. Evidence of this long history of intermarriage with Mestizo and indigenous Mexicans is also expressed in the fact that in the 2015 inter-census, 64.9% (896,829) of Afro-Mexicans also identified as indigenous. It was also reported that 9.3% of Afro-Mexicans speak an indigenous language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup> The states with the highest self-report of Afro-Mexicans were Guerrero (6.5% of the population), Oaxaca (4.95%) and Veracruz (3.28%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup> Afro-Mexican culture is strongest in the communities of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Costa_Chica_of_Oaxaca" title="Costa Chica of Oaxaca">Costa Chica of Oaxaca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Costa_Chica_of_Guerrero" title="Costa Chica of Guerrero">Costa Chica of Guerrero</a>.</p>
<p>Smaller ethnic groups in Mexico include <a href="/wiki/Asian_Mexicans" title="Asian Mexicans">South and East Asians</a>, present since the colonial era. During the colonial era Asians were termed <i>Chino</i> (regardless of ethnicity), and arrived as merchants, artisans and slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seijas_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seijas-265">[265]</a></sup> The largest group were Filipinos and some 200,000 Mexicans can trace <a href="/wiki/Filipino_immigration_to_Mexico" title="Filipino immigration to Mexico">Filipino ancestry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Modern Asian immigration began in the late 19th century and at one point in the early 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_immigration_to_Mexico" title="Chinese immigration to Mexico">Chinese</a> were the second largest immigrant group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> During the early 20th century, a substantial number of <a href="/wiki/Arab_Mexicans" title="Arab Mexicans">Arabs</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Arab_Christians" title="Arab Christians">Christians</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup> began arriving from the crumbling <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. The largest group were the Lebanese and an estimated 400,000 Mexicans have some <a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Mexicans" title="Lebanese Mexicans">Lebanese ancestry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_censuses">Official censuses</span></h4>
<p>The first census in Mexico that included an ethnic classification was the 1793 census. Also known as the Revillagigedo census, it was Mexico's (then known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">New Spain</a>) first national population census. Most of its original datasets have reportedly been lost, thus most of what is known about it nowadays comes from essays and field investigations made by academics who had access to the census data and used it as reference for their works such as Prussian geographer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a>. While every author gives different estimations for each racial group in the country they don't seem to vary much, with Europeans ranging from 18% to 22% of New Spain's population, Mestizos ranging from 21% to 25%, Indians ranging from 51% to 61% and Africans being between 6,000 and 10,000, The estimations given for the total population range from 3,799,561 to 6,122,354. It is concluded then, that across nearly three centuries of colonization, the population growth trends of whites and mestizos were even, while the total percentage of the indigenous population decreased at a rate of 13%–17% per century. The authors assert that rather than whites and mestizos having higher birthrates, the reason for the indigenous population's numbers decreasing lies on them suffering of higher mortality rates, due living in remote locations rather than on cities and towns founded by the Spanish colonists or being at war with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup> Anthropologist <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gonzalo_Aguirre_Beltr%C3%A1n" title="es:Gonzalo Aguirre Beltrán">Gonzalo Aguirre Beltrán</a> goes beyond said numbers and splits the Mestizo group into "Euromestizos", "Indomestizos" and "Afromestizos" calculating their numbers on more than one million, 700,000 and 600,000 respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aguirre_Beltr.C3.A1n_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aguirre_Beltr.C3.A1n-271">[271]</a></sup> Independent-era Mexico eliminated the legal basis of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castas" title="Castas">Colonial caste system</a> which led to exclude racial classification in the censuses to come.</p>
<p>According to Mexico's second census ever which considered race, made right after the Mexican revolution on 1921,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somosprimos.com_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-somosprimos.com-272">[272]</a></sup> 59% of Mexico's population was Mestizo, 29% was Indigenous and only 9% was European, with Mestizos being the most numerous ethno-racial group in almost all the states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somosprimos.com_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-somosprimos.com-272">[272]</a></sup> For a long time this census' results have been taken as fact, with extraofficial international publications such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">The World Factbook</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a> using them as a reference to estimate Mexico's racial composition up to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Factbook_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Factbook-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica_248-2"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclop.C3.A6dia_Britannica-248">[248]</a></sup> However, in recent time Mexican academics have subjected the census' results to scrutiny, claiming that such a drastic alteration on demographic trends in regards to the 1793 census is not possible and cite, among other statistics the relatively low frequency of marriages between people of different continental ancestries in colonial and early independent Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Said authors claim that the Mexican society went through a "more cultural than biological mestizaje process" sponsored by the state on its efforts to unify the Mexican population which resulted on the inflation of the percentage of the Mestizo Mexican group at the expense of the identity of the other races that exist in Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent times the Mexican government has decided to conduct ethnic surveys and censuses again, and has also widen the criteria to classify the ethnicies who were already considered, an example being the Indigenous Mexican classification, which was previously reserved to people who lived in indigenous communities and/or spoke an indigenous language. According to these recent surveys Indigenous peoples amount to 21.5% of Mexico's population (including people who declared to be partially indigenous),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beta.inegi.org.mx_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-beta.inegi.org.mx-252">[252]</a></sup> Afro-Mexicans are 1.2% of Mexico's population (including people who declared to be partially African)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beta.inegi.org.mx_252-2"><a href="#cite_note-beta.inegi.org.mx-252">[252]</a></sup> and European Mexicans amount to 47% of Mexico's population (based on appearance rather than on self-declared of ancestry).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marzo_DiaIntElimDiscRacial_INACCSS_2017_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marzo_DiaIntElimDiscRacial_INACCSS_2017-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conapred.org.mx_257-1"><a href="#cite_note-conapred.org.mx-257">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DISC-RACIAL_2011_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-DISC-RACIAL_2011-258">[258]</a></sup> Less numerous groups in Mexico such as Asians and Middle Easterners are also accounted for, albeit their numbers do not vary significantly from previous estimations. Out of all the ethnic groups that have recently been surveyed, that of Mestizos is notably absent, which may be consequence of the ethnic label's fluid and subjective definition, which complicates a precise calculation as well the tendency that Mexicans have to identify people with "static" ethnic labels rather than "fluid" ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[276]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Mexico" title="Languages of Mexico">Languages of Mexico</a></div>
<p>The country has the largest Spanish-speaking population in the world with almost a third of all Spanish native speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spanishhistory_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spanishhistory-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup></p>
<p>Almost all of the Mexican population speaks Spanish, 99.3% according to the latest census;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> nonetheless around 5.4% still speaks an indigenous language besides Spanish. The indigenous languages with the most speakers are <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a>, spoken by approximately 1.45 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yukatek_Maya" title="Yukatek Maya">Yukatek Maya</a> spoken by some 750,000 people and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixtec_languages" title="Mixtec languages">Mixtec</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Zapotec_languages" title="Zapotec languages">Zapotec languages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> each spoken by more than 400,000 people.</p>
<p>The National Institute of Indigenous Languages <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/INALI" title="INALI">INALI</a> recognizes 68 linguistic groups and some 364 different specific varieties of indigenous languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> Since the promulgation of the <a href="/wiki/Ley_General_de_Derechos_Ling%C3%BC%C3%ADsticos_de_los_Pueblos_Ind%C3%ADgenas" title="Ley General de Derechos Lingüísticos de los Pueblos Indígenas">Law of Indigenous Linguistic Rights</a> in 2003, these languages have had status as national languages, with equal validity with Spanish in all the areas and contexts in which they are spoken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the indigenous languages, other minority languages are spoken by immigrant populations, such as the 80,000 German-speaking <a href="/wiki/Mennonites_in_Mexico" title="Mennonites in Mexico">Mennonites in Mexico</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> and 5,000 speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Chipilo_Venetian_dialect" title="Chipilo Venetian dialect">Chipilo dialect</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Venetian_language" title="Venetian language">Venetian language</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Chipilo" title="Chipilo">Chipilo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puebla" title="Puebla">Puebla</a>.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> is the most commonly spoken foreign language in Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_areas">Urban areas</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_areas_of_Mexico" title="Metropolitan areas of Mexico">Metropolitan areas of Mexico</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Mexico" title="List of cities in Mexico">List of cities in Mexico</a></div>
<p>Here are the 20 largest urban areas in Mexico.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Mexico" title="Religion in Mexico">Religion in Mexico</a></div>
